<div id="review_1391168006" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1391168006" />
    <a title="Krista Claudine Baetiong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong"><img alt="Krista Claudine Baetiong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552474554p2/13624574.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1391168006?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista Claudine Baetiong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista Claudine Baetiong" href="/user/show/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong">Krista Claudine Baetiong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=author-north-america">author-north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=period-medieval">period-medieval</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1391168006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16986891511269419835"><i>Today, April 6, 2016 (we're hours ahead on the time zone), mark the 817th death anniversary of Richard I of England, famously known as the “Lionheart” for his legendary military skills and daring exploits. I am reposting my review on the book Lionheart by Sharon Kay Penman, which I read last year. :-)</i><br/><br/>One word that can justifiably illustrate King Richard I, and that Sharon Kay Penman has used in this book thrice: <i>bravura</i>. Indeed, he was never short of audacity, always leading his men to the front</span>
  <span id="freeText16986891511269419835" style="display:none"><i>Today, April 6, 2016 (we're hours ahead on the time zone), mark the 817th death anniversary of Richard I of England, famously known as the “Lionheart” for his legendary military skills and daring exploits. I am reposting my review on the book Lionheart by Sharon Kay Penman, which I read last year. :-)</i><br /><br />One word that can justifiably illustrate King Richard I, and that Sharon Kay Penman has used in this book thrice: <i>bravura</i>. Indeed, he was never short of audacity, always leading his men to the front line and proving himself a capable military tactician as early as sixteen. For all his blunders as a son, a husband and a king, or even the risky exploits he had committed himself into that almost bordered on perverse tenacity and perhaps a strong disregard to his self-preservation, no one could ever dispute his sound warrior skills and great courage in the battlefield. Reading this book felt like watching a far-fetched movie about a hero who is glorified excessively for his ability to run through the enemy line on his own, come to his wife and sister’s rescue in the nick of time and in a rather dramatic fashion, and reclaim a besieged town and hold fast to it even when they were greatly outnumbered by the enemy—only, these aren’t merely whimsical hero’s tales but a few of the many testimonies that proved Richard’s legendary valor.<br /><br />No wonder he’d been referred to as the “Lionheart” in his lifetime and thereafter. He was definitely a force to reckon with, a “medieval rockstar”, to quote one book reviewer. So admirable was his authority that many were enamored to follow him to hell and back and sacrificed themselves on his behalf, like one Norman knight did. I think I would do the same if I were part of his retinue; I would also stay loyal and trusting and confident even to be sleeping soundly every night knowing that my liege lord could easily outdo the saints in performing miracles and bringing good fortune in his favor every time. Sadly, though, that with all the illustriousness surrounding King Richard’s life, his death had come out rather tragically lackluster (actually, his later years and death aren't covered in this book, all the more reason to read the next—and final— installment of Penman’s Plantagenet series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18079806.A_King_s_Ransom__Plantagenets__5_" title="A King's Ransom (Plantagenets #5) by Sharon Kay Penman" rel="nofollow noopener">A King's Ransom</a>).<br /><br />I detested his selfish and skeptical nature in the previous book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2859559.Devil_s_Brood__Henry_II___Eleanor_of_Aquitaine___3_" title="Devil's Brood (Henry II &amp; Eleanor of Aquitaine, #3) by Sharon Kay Penman" rel="nofollow noopener">Devil's Brood</a>, although my general sentiment about him after reading this story has now quite mellowed to ambivalence (or even perhaps a bit more on the approving side). Penman has painted King Richard here in a different light—a pleasant kind of different in my opinion, and a satisfying divergence from the many chronicles practically underlining his disreputable deeds. I also appreciate how the author has narrated the events of the Third Crusade in a very detailed fashion that didn’t turn out flat in the end. I find that she has a witty way of describing historical events, and if she were a History professor, she’d surely have lots of eager enrolees in her class!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16986891511269419835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_221991305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/221991305" />
    <a title="Lori Anderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/382208-lori-anderson"><img alt="Lori Anderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555549941p2/382208.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/221991305?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lori Anderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lori Anderson" href="/user/show/382208-lori-anderson">Lori Anderson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">historical fiction fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/382208-lori-anderson?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer221991305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9727780084211513395">I want to caveat my Three Star rating by saying I adore Sharon Kay Penman. I pre-ordered this book and interrupted what I was currently reading (an unheard of thing for me!) to start "Lionheart". While I wasn't sorry I did that, and wasn't at ALL sorry I read this book, to me, it wasn't like her others.<br/><br/><br/>Richard the Lionheart is a noteworthy, larger-than-life person in history I was dying to learn more about. This particular book covers the crusades, another section of history I didn't know much a</span>
  <span id="freeText9727780084211513395" style="display:none">I want to caveat my Three Star rating by saying I adore Sharon Kay Penman. I pre-ordered this book and interrupted what I was currently reading (an unheard of thing for me!) to start "Lionheart". While I wasn't sorry I did that, and wasn't at ALL sorry I read this book, to me, it wasn't like her others.<br /><br /><br />Richard the Lionheart is a noteworthy, larger-than-life person in history I was dying to learn more about. This particular book covers the crusades, another section of history I didn't know much about. I learned quite a lot about the Crusades, and learned it in a way that will stick with me and was far richer and more interesting than reading a dry history text. However, one of the things I love about Penman's books are her richly developed characters, and I felt that was missing in "Lionheart". Just when I thought a character was on the verge of opening up and blossoming into a richer, more intensely interesting person, Penman moves on to something else. <br /><br /><br />This minor character development didn't ruin the story for me, though. She DID develop the characters well enough, and in some scenes, QUITE well, to make this book a page-turner. I just missed her usual style. I have to admit, I don't have any idea how any other author out there could have tackled this subject any better, so I give the book Three Stars, meaning yes, I liked it, and yes, all Sharon Kay Penman fans should read it. But a new reader who discovers Penman should start with "The Sunne in Splendour" and then her series from the first book and not skip around.<br /><br /><br />Looking forward to her next book!!!<br /><br /><br />Lori Anderson<br /><br /><a href="http://www.prettythingsblog.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a><br /><a href="http://www.lorianderson.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Shop</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9727780084211513395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2330515284720964792">4,5 stars<br/><br/>I have to admit I’ve never tried to read a book about Richard whilst he was on crusade. I feared it would be boring but it certainly wasn’t. I confess I had a crush on him in Devil’s Brood so I was so excited that this book came out.<br/><br/>Have to feel sorry for Berengaria though, even if the marriage started well. They were so ill-matched that I’m surprised it started that well at all. Sometimes opposites attract and all that but sometimes it’s just too different.<br/><br/>I really liked Joanna and how</span>
  <span id="freeText2330515284720964792" style="display:none">4,5 stars<br /><br />I have to admit I’ve never tried to read a book about Richard whilst he was on crusade. I feared it would be boring but it certainly wasn’t. I confess I had a crush on him in Devil’s Brood so I was so excited that this book came out.<br /><br />Have to feel sorry for Berengaria though, even if the marriage started well. They were so ill-matched that I’m surprised it started that well at all. Sometimes opposites attract and all that but sometimes it’s just too different.<br /><br />I really liked Joanna and how she could knock some sense into her brother’s head at times. It seems like it’s been too long since I read Devil’s Brood and I tried to remember if we met Joanna in that book also? I can’t remember.<br /><br />It’s not a fast read but I didn’t find it boring. There’s a huge cast of characters and POV changes that might be annoying to some readers.</span>

  <span id="freeText7402924311272472542" style="display:none">I gave up on page 77.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236594957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1345433720729783724">This tale encompasses a wide cast of characters centered on the Crusades and Richard the Lionheart.<br/>It's a great overview of the period IMO though for people who don't like long historical tales this will drag in certain parts.<br/><br/>The voice narrator does an awesome job with the many voices. Part of the appeal.<br/><br/><b> MY GRADE: B minus to B. </b></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_526264962" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526264962" />
    <a title="Paula Lofting" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5771806-paula-lofting"><img alt="Paula Lofting" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442636019p2/5771806.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/526264962?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paula Lofting" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paula Lofting" href="/user/show/5771806-paula-lofting">Paula Lofting</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780230764781" href="/book/show/13159489-lionheart">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526264962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16262456222438944592">It has taken me awhile to get to grips with how I will write this review. I guess I should start by saying that I have always in the past enjoyed this author's books but for some reason, this book did not have the same wow factor I got when I read the others. This could be for a number of reasons; one being that since I have started writing myself, I have a more discerning eye, or I have simply out grown Ms Penman's style and lean more towards the rather more masculine re-telling of history rath</span>
  <span id="freeText16262456222438944592" style="display:none">It has taken me awhile to get to grips with how I will write this review. I guess I should start by saying that I have always in the past enjoyed this author's books but for some reason, this book did not have the same wow factor I got when I read the others. This could be for a number of reasons; one being that since I have started writing myself, I have a more discerning eye, or I have simply out grown Ms Penman's style and lean more towards the rather more masculine re-telling of history rather than the romantic slant that some authors put on their novels. The last book I remember reading of Ms Penman's was Devil's Brood 6 years ago. I remember then wishing that there was more action in the book, but I loved it nonetheless. Since then, my tastes in writing style has obviously changed -a lot, as I was to find out when I read Lionheart.<br /><br />For me, the problem started early. I was irritated that I had to wade through the scenes with women discussing various things about sex, babies and love etc to page 70 before Richard - who is Lionheart appears. To me this was baffling. When he does appear, suddenly my interest is ignited and I am totally there. The scenes that followed were more masculine, and I was enjoying it immensely only to want more and then suddenly the scene and point of view would change and I am left feeling unfulfilled. There was a curious scene in which William Marshall and his wife open it from their point of view for about a paragraph, maybe more and then it suddenly changes to someone else's point of view. The Marshalls don't appear ever again throughout the book which felt somewhat bizarre. Why bother to put them in for a few lines in the limelight and then not appear again - ever? It seemed odd. <br /><br />But that is the author's style, she switches point of views throughout the book on numerous occasions and head hopping is rife throughout the book. Whilst this is not always a bad thing, and i know it is the author's style, but for me it made it difficult to feel that Richard was the main character of the book and that perhaps the title should have been something else.<br /><br />But this book was not without its greatness. Ms Penman is faultless in her research of this subject. Her descriptions of the places and the way she creates a visual ambiance so vividly are second to none. At times, she crafts her words into the stuff that makes be have a sharp intake of breath. At other times the language used was a little anachronistic sounding, a little too modern.<br />the characters were many, and at times developing nicely but even the main character, Lionheart himself, was overshadowed by the supporting characters who were given far too much attention. I think the author would have done better to have written it in a sort of Game of thrones style where everyone has time devoted to them and titled it, as someone else has mentioned in their review, Lionheart's Crusade.<br /><br />Overall, I liked this book, which is why I am giving it 3.5 stars, but mainly for the quality of research and the vast undertaking. To write this topic in such detail is an amazing feat and despite the niggles I had with the style, the head-hopping and constant POV changes, it is a worthy book. There were times, like Richard's stand in the besieged city (I think it was Jaffa) that the writing was so good, I wished for more of it.<br /><br /> 3.5 stars<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16262456222438944592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_191808139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/191808139" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3739451-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352731541p2/3739451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/191808139?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/3739451-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3739451-suzanne?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3739451-suzanne?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer191808139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1721867718588614801">Historical fiction doesn’t get any better than this. I had to admit, when I heard this book was about King Richard the Lionheart’s crusade to wrest Jerusalem from the Saracens, I was skeptical as to how good this book could possibly be. I pictured nothing but boredom, marches and battles, with a lot of killing and maiming thrown in. What I found when I actually read the novel, was an amazing tale of heroism, political intrigue, back-stabbing, and yes, marches and battles with a lot of killing an</span>
  <span id="freeText1721867718588614801" style="display:none">Historical fiction doesn’t get any better than this. I had to admit, when I heard this book was about King Richard the Lionheart’s crusade to wrest Jerusalem from the Saracens, I was skeptical as to how good this book could possibly be. I pictured nothing but boredom, marches and battles, with a lot of killing and maiming thrown in. What I found when I actually read the novel, was an amazing tale of heroism, political intrigue, back-stabbing, and yes, marches and battles with a lot of killing and maiming. However, Penman is so adept at describing the military events that the reader cannot help being totally engrossed. The story is so good, in fact, that I started to question whether it could actually be true. Never fear, as usual, Sharon Kay Penman is quite the stickler for historical accuracy. In her notes at the end of the book, she carefully explains any creative license she uses – and that is, of course, very little.<br /><br />I also greatly enjoyed seeing Richard being played in a different light – more likeable, than past portrayals of the larger than life King. Again, Penman backs up her arguments with painstaking research. It is refreshing and exciting to discover something new about a man and events that happened so long ago.<br /><br />Bravo to Ms. Penman, for another wonderful novel. I eagerly await the release of <i>A King’s Ransom</i> – her next and final foray into the world of Richard the Lionheart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1721867718588614801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_385977057" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/385977057" />
    <a title="Anna Pearce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/114657-anna-pearce"><img alt="Anna Pearce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1204052645p2/114657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/385977057?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Pearce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Pearce" href="/user/show/114657-anna-pearce">Anna Pearce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/114657-anna-pearce?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer385977057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11557154155014847145">Summary of Book: Richard the Lionheart, son of Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine, goes on Crusade. There is strife. Richard is the bravest man to ever brave. Richard does not manage to win because the French are treacherous. Richard is sad, and returns to England. Since this is a very long book, that's a very short summary. There's lots in the middle.<br/><br/>Summary of my feelings reading this book: Interesting! Interesting! Ooh, that's probably not accurate. Interesting! Oh look, the wars are starting.</span>
  <span id="freeText11557154155014847145" style="display:none">Summary of Book: Richard the Lionheart, son of Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine, goes on Crusade. There is strife. Richard is the bravest man to ever brave. Richard does not manage to win because the French are treacherous. Richard is sad, and returns to England. Since this is a very long book, that's a very short summary. There's lots in the middle.<br /><br />Summary of my feelings reading this book: Interesting! Interesting! Ooh, that's probably not accurate. Interesting! Oh look, the wars are starting. Bored bored bored bored bored bored bored, interlude where something other than war happens, exciting! bored bored bored bored bored. The end. Still bored.<br /><br />Generally I've liked most of Penman's stuff (I've not read her mysteries) but when she decides she's going to write about a war that actually takes place on the battlefield her stuff gets plodding and difficult and frightfully <em>dull</em>. <em>Lionheart</em> takes place almost entirely on the battlefield (much like <em>When Christ and His Saint's Slept</em>, the other Penman book I hate) and it feels like something I need to plod through so the next book (<em>A King's Ransom</em>) will make sense. Frankly, I should have just read Wikipedia's article on the Third Crusade and been done with it. The only other thing I think I needed to know was that she's (of course) included the offspring of her fictional Welsh Gary Stu character, who just happens to be present at all of the major things that she needs someone to be present for.<br /><br />The book also suffers from many of the other problems that Penman's works do. If someone likes or admires Eleanor of Aquitaine, they're "good"; if they distrust her or treat her with disdain for being an uppity woman, they're "bad". Men we are to like are always kind and romantic towards their wives, with the common mention of them satisfying these women sexually. Unkind men are bad. (Their wives are always long-suffering.) Many of the main characters show a remarkable amount of religious tolerance - an acceptance and even appreciation for Jewish people during a time of heavy persecution of Jews, for example. Less sympathetic characters can be talked into considering Jewish people to be good; evil characters distrust everyone who would ever consider such a thing. I don't know, it's just kinda tiring how easy it is to assign people a side in these books, as though the whole historic situation isn't a big complicated mess of conflicting things. (It's not that I want the average heroine to be anti-Semitic, it's more that even the background characters who hardly ghost across the page have the same check box morality. It's the Crusades, for crying out loud! I'm expecting <em>someone</em> other than the hated French to say "Gosh, these non-Christian people are suspicious characters, what with the years-long war and all.")<br /><br />What I generally like about Penman's works is the way that she draws the family conflicts out, puts them on center stage, and makes them the key reason for events. What I love about her earlier works focusing on Eleanor of Aquitaine, on Henry II, on John, on Richard III, is how much they're small family dramas played out by people with huge amounts of power and armies at their beck and call. The falling apart of Eleanor and Henry's marriage literally rips apart a kingdom, and the reader inevitably ends up feeling the whole thing makes a painful sort of sense. <br /><br />Without that emotional bond, though, her novels just don't work for me. Both <em>Saints</em> and <em>Lionheart</em> just move characters from one battle to another. While I have no doubt they're historically accurate in the details, they don't make great reading.<br /><br />I am looking forward to the next one, though. It looks like family drama will once again rule the book, and that's the Penman I love. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11557154155014847145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_310309304" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/310309304" />
    <a title="Angela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8711587-angela"><img alt="Angela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863713p2/8711587.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/310309304?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/8711587-angela">Angela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8711587-angela?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8711587-angela?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8711587-angela?shelf=five-star-reads">five-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer310309304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7087909148195745838">I have finally finished. I really enjoyed this book and Sharon Kay Penman is the master of medieval historical fiction. It turned on its head many misconstrued ideas I had in relation to Richard the Lionheart. I like him a lot more now. I love how tactical and strategic he was. Being an Angevin no doubt helped this. After all with Eleanor Aquitaine as a mother you had to have some diplomacy skills. <br/><br/>The book centres around Richard's involvement in the Third Crusade to Outremer and what a journey </span>
  <span id="freeText7087909148195745838" style="display:none">I have finally finished. I really enjoyed this book and Sharon Kay Penman is the master of medieval historical fiction. It turned on its head many misconstrued ideas I had in relation to Richard the Lionheart. I like him a lot more now. I love how tactical and strategic he was. Being an Angevin no doubt helped this. After all with Eleanor Aquitaine as a mother you had to have some diplomacy skills. <br /><br />The book centres around Richard's involvement in the Third Crusade to Outremer and what a journey is was to get there. From Phillipe Capet trying to block him at every turn, it is a wonder that the French and the English even made it to Outremer without killing each other. We spend the first half of the novel with Richard trying to get Phillipe to even commit to going on Crusade, even though they have both vowed to. It is obvious from the outset that Phillipe doesn't want to go on Crusade but eventually he succumbs and the journey begins, from France to Sicily to Cyprus and finally Outrember. Along the way it is a constant battle as both Sicily and Cyprus are in turmoil.<br /><br />Finally arriving in Outremer, it is battle to rescue Jerusalem from the the Saracens. It is easy to forget how fervently people believed in God and that it dictated so much of daily life in medieval times. The zeal that men were prepared to die and the desire to rescue Jerusalem are plain to see. Both sides really believed they were doing the will of God. The Saracens protecting their religious sites and the Crusaders trying to retrieve them. <br /><br />Saladin is clearly portrayed as a compassion man. He sends Richard fruit and snow when he is seriously ill and there is always the sense of him being honourable. Saladin is often in the background though as this is Richard's story.<br /><br />Richard was a man of war. He comes across as one of the great warriors (if not the greatest) of his time and his prowess on the battlefield was legendary. There are numerous instances where he seemingly recklessly threw himself into battle, yet he always found a way to survive. He would have been very formidable if you were staring at him across a battlefied.<br /><br />The books is also a depiction of how long things took. We are so use to being able to board a plane or drive a car to get somewhere. In medieval times it could take months. When Richard receives news it is months later. While he fights the Saracens, he receives troubling news from home and he battles with the desire to do God's work and to maintain his kingdom.<br /><br />This book is only part 1 or 2. Penman stated she had too much material to complete just one novel and I can't wait to read part 2 when Richard returns from Crusade, as he already knows that his brother John is plotting against him and the Phillipe Capet is trying to take over his domains. This books is must for lovers of medieval fiction. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7087909148195745838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;

    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UrcZMEBGnKQcc1BdVJwDycypMCaZ2m4NhLTZAy5kckfqW+BwtbV02H96L1/ZCnKA0+gy6xJ/VJm50lVd4gRCmw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('h0j1h8x+rbB2amp0zkcLeQpT+6p+2BRpSY6QifAaWkw/pAzHOY1FzBVjFXZD0XowFRL5Z/V9Lv106BzXPHpqkA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Defying her powerful cousin Elizabeth I, Mary set sail in 1561 to take her place as the Catholic Queen of a newly Protestant Scotland. A virtual stranger in her volatile native land, Mary would be hailed as a saint, denounced as a whore, and ultimately accused of murdering her second husband, Lord Darnley, in order to marry her lover, the Earl of Bothwell. She was but twenty-five years old when she fled Scotland for the imagined sanctuary of Elizabeth\'s England, where she would be embroiled in intrigue until she was beheaded &quot;like a criminal&quot; in 1587. In her stunning first novel, The Autobiography of Henry VIII, Margaret George established herself as one of the finest historical novelists of our time. Now she brings us a new, mesmerizing blend of history and storytelling as she turns the astonishing facts of the life of Mary Queen of Scots into magnificent fiction that sweeps us from the glittering French court where Mary spent her youth, to the bloodstained Scotland where she reigned as Queen, to the cold English castles where she ended her days. From the moment he catches sight of Judith, daughter of the King’s formidable sister, he knows he has found his future wife.When Waltheof saves Judith’s life, it is clear that the attraction is mutual. But marriage has little to do with love in medieval Europe. When William refuses to let the couple wed, Waltheof joins forces with his fellow rebels in an uprising against the King. William brutally crushes the rebellion, but realizes that Waltheof cannot be ignored. Marrying him to his niece, he decides, is the perfect way to keep him in check.But is the match between the Saxon earl and Norman lady made in heaven or hell? As their children grow, Waltheof and Judith must choose between their feelings for each other and older loyalties. At the same time, the reputation of Waltheof’s Norman acquaintance Simon de Senlis continues to flourish. The son of William’s chamberlain, he shares a special bond with Waltheof, who rescued him from being trampled by a horse when he was a squire. Now Simon enjoys the confidence of both the King and the rebel earl. And when tension between the two ignites once more, it is Simon who is set to reap the reward.Based on an astonishing true story of honor, treachery, and love, The Winter Mantle is historical fiction It was a time when old certainties had been shredded: by popular insurgency, economic crisis, feuding and a corrupt, bankrupt government presided over by the imbecilic, Lancastrian King Henry VI. The country was in need of a new hero. Magnetic, narcissistic, Edward found himself on the throne, and alongside him his two younger brothers: the unstable, petulant George, Duke of Clarence, and the boy who would emerge from his shadow, Richard, Duke of Gloucester.\n\nCharismatic, able and ambitious, the brothers would become the figureheads of a spectacular ruling dynasty, one that laid the foundations for a renewal of English royal power. Yet a web of grudges and resentments grew between them, generating a destructive sequence of conspiracy, rebellion, deposition, fratricide, usurpation and regicide. The house of York\'s brutal end came on 22August 1485 at Bosworth Field, with the death of the youngest brother, now Richard III, at the hands of a new usurper, Henry Tudor.\n\nBrothers York is the story of three remarkable brothers, two of whom were crowned kings of England and the other an heir presumptive, whose antagonism was fuelled by the mistrust and vendettas of the age that brought their family to power. The house of York should have been the dynasty that the Tudors became. Its tragedy was that it devoured itself.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15491374772529880024\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/24655.Sharon_Kay_Penman">About Sharon Kay Penman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/24655.Sharon_Kay_Penman"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1234237583p3/24655.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/24655.Sharon_Kay_Penman">Sharon Kay Penman
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,429

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17404755198076926917">Penman received her bachelor's degree from the University of Texas at Austin, she majored in history, and also received a Juris Doctor (J.D.) degree from Rutgers University School of Law, and later worked as a tax lawyer. <br/><br/>The Sunne in Splendour, a novel about Richard III of England is one of the most popular books on the Historical Novel Society's list of best historical novels. In 1996, following</span>
  <span id="freeText17404755198076926917" style="display:none">Penman received her bachelor's degree from the University of Texas at Austin, she majored in history, and also received a Juris Doctor (J.D.) degree from Rutgers University School of Law, and later worked as a tax lawyer. <br /><br />The Sunne in Splendour, a novel about Richard III of England is one of the most popular books on the Historical Novel Society's list of best historical novels. In 1996, following the success of When Christ and His Saints Slept (which dealt with the Anarchy and the early career of King Henry II of England), Penman ventured into the historical whodunnit with four mysteries set in the court of Eleanor of Aquitaine during the reign of Richard I. The mysteries did not enjoy the same success as her "straight" historical novels, to which she returned in 2002, with Time and Chance, again covering the life of Eleanor of Aquitaine and Henry II. In 2008, she published Devil's Brood, which was to be the final book in her trilogy about Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine. She soon realized that there was still more of the Angevins' story to tell and the result was Lionheart , followed by The King's Ransom. Henry and Eleanor's celebrated and controversial son, Richard the Lionheart is the major character in both books, although Eleanor, John, and Richard's favorite sister, Joanna, also get to spend time on center stage. She has just finished The Land Beyond the Sea, set in the Kingdom of Jerusalem in the late 12th century. It will be published in the U.S. by G.P. Putnam's and in the U.K. and Down Under by Macmillan and co; the publication date is early March, 2020. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17404755198076926917" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_2859559'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2859559-devil-s-brood?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Devil&#39;s Brood  (Henry II &amp; Eleanor of Aquitaine, #3)<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/24655.Sharon_Kay_Penman?origin=series_works\">Sharon Kay Penman<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.38 avg rating &mdash; 6,861 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2008\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17506131769465447731\">The long-awaited and highly anticipated final volume in Penman’s trilogy of Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine—a tumultuous conclusion to this timeless story of love, power, ambition, and betrayal. Where the second novel in the trilogy, Time And Chanc<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17506131769465447731\" style=\"display:none\">The long-awaited and highly anticipated final volume in Penman’s trilogy of Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine—a tumultuous conclusion to this timeless story of love, power, ambition, and betrayal. Where the second novel in the trilogy, Time And Chance, dealt with the extraordinary politics of the twelfth century, climaxing with the murder of Thomas Becket and Henry’s confrontation with the Church and self-imposed exile to Ireland, Devil’s Brood centers on the implosion of a family. And because it is a royal family whose domains span the English Channel and whose alliances encompass the Christian world, that collapse will have dire consequences. This is a story of betrayal as Henry’s three eldest sons and his wife enter into a rebellion against him, aligning themselves with his bitterest enemy, King Louis of France. But it is also the story of a great king whose brilliance forged an empire but whose personal blind spots led him into the most serious mistake of his life. Sharon Kay Penman has created a novel of tremendous power, as two strong-willed, passionate people clash, a family divides, and a marriage ends in all but name. Curiously, it is a novel without villains—only flawed human beings caught up in misperceptions and bad judgment calls. Most devastating to Henry was not his sons’ rebellion but his wife’s betrayal in joining them. How could it happen that two people whose love for each other was all consuming end up as bitter adversaries? That is the heart of Penman’s tale in Devil’s Brood.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17506131769465447731\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n     n = load.length,

        script.src = load[i];
        first.parentNode.insertBefore(script, first);

  } else {
    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
